  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_25bc4681-6b3e-496a-8e97-b78e9edac271'
      });
    }

      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"

  <span id="freeText4281150154011599341" style="display:none">The Culture - a humanoid/machine symbiotic society - has thrown up many great Game Players. One of the best is Jernau Morat Gurgeh, Player of Games, master of every board, computer and strategy. Bored with success, Gurgeh travels to the Empire of Azad, cruel &amp; incredibly wealthy, to try their fabulous game, a game so complex, so like life itself, that the winner becomes emperor. Mocked, blackmailed, almost murdered, Gurgeh accepts the game and with it the challenge of his life, and very possibly his death.</span>

            var lessLink = e.next();
            swapContent(lessLink);
          }
        });

<a href="/questions/394230-considering-that-the-descriptions-vary">Considering that the descriptions vary wildly... do books one and two actually connect are are they just set in the same universe? Both look good, but I read the preview of this in the back of K.J. Parker&#39;s Escapement and I&#39;ve been itching to find out about this mysterious &quot;Gurgeh&quot;</a>

<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">You can read book two without having read book one. A lot of facets of Culture in book one are not brought up or particularly relevant in book two. Ba<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">You can read book two without having read book one. A lot of facets of Culture in book one are not brought up or particularly relevant in book two. Banks explains, in book two, what you need to know about the characters and systems in order to understand that book.<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>

<div class='communityAnswer'>
<div class='communityAnswerBody'>
<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/17149370-austin-hennessey"><img alt="" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/users/1537310029i/17149370._UX100_CR0,12,100,100_.jpg" /></a>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/17149370-austin-hennessey">Austin Hennessey</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<div class='spoiler'>
This answer contains spoilers…
<span class='spoilerAnswerText'>
<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[
  
    The Player of Games Character List<br />1.	Jernau Morat Gurgeh: The player of games who is the main protagonist of the book. He is a member of the Culture a<a href="#" rel="nofollow noopener">…more</a>
    The Player of Games Character List<br />1.	Jernau Morat Gurgeh: The player of games who is the main protagonist of the book. He is a member of the Culture and is from the Chiark Orbital. A post-human who is skilled at playing games but is getting bored with his life because playing games isn’t as much fun anymore especially since in the world of the Culture there is no longer scarcity. Betting on games is rendered pointless and does not add any thrill to playing the games. <br />2.	Mahwrin Skel: A droid that used to be a member of Special Circumstances but was taken off due to behavioral problems. He blackmails Gurgeh into helping him get back onto the Special Circumstances by having him convince Contact that he is worthy of the position. <br />3.	Contact: Responsible for The Culture’s interactions (diplomatic or otherwise) with other civilizations (though non-Contact citizens are apparently not prevented from travelling or interacting with other civilizations). <br />4.	Special Circumstances: Further within Contact, an intelligence organization named Special Circumstances exists to deal with interventions which require more covert behavior; the interventionist approach that the Culture takes to advancing other societies may often create resentment in the affected civilizations and thus requires a rather delicate touch.<br />5.	Chamlis Amalkney: An old female droid that is Gurgeh’s long time friend. Gives advice to Gurgeh a couple of times and gives a gift to him on his departure to Azad.<br />6.	Yay Meristinoux: One of Gurgeh’s friends who Gurgeh may have a romantic interest in.<br />7.	Worthil: A representative from Contact who explains to Gurgeh the game of Azad and why they need him to go. <br />8.	Haflis: Man (undergoes multiple sex changes) who throws parties and hosted Stricken game <br />9.	Olz Hap: Young game player who is good at Stricken and plays Gurgeh. <br />10.	Boruelal: A professor at a university. She is tall, attractive an twice gurgehs age. <br />11.	Shuro: A fan of Gurgeh’s game playing. He is best at the game called Deploy. <br />12.	Flere-Imsaho: A small droid that accompanies Gurgeh on the planet Eä that gives him advice on their culture and also translates for Gurgeh between Eächic and Marain. <br />13.	The limiting factor: the ship to take him to Eä<br />14.	The little rascal: the ship that tool him from the culture to the limiting factor <br />15.	Pequil: <br />16.	Shohobohaum Za: <br />17.	The Priest: <br />18.	Lo Shav Olos: <br />19.	Lo Wescekibold Ram: <br />20.	Lo Prinest Bermoiya: <br />21.	Yomonul:<br />22.	Nicosar: The emperor of Azad who lives on Echrondeal with the rest of the government<br /><br /><a href="#" rel="nofollow noopener">(less)</a>
  
 <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>
</span>
</div>

<div id="review_2319567412" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2319567412" />
    <a title="Mario the lone bookwolf (semi reviewing hiatus ) " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/78485297-mario-the-lone-bookwolf-semi-reviewing-hiatus"><img alt="Mario the lone bookwolf (semi reviewing hiatus ) " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1588320656p2/78485297.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2319567412?book_show_action=true">Mar 07, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mario the lone bookwolf (semi reviewing hiatus ) " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mario the lone bookwolf (semi reviewing hiatus ) " href="/user/show/78485297-mario-the-lone-bookwolf-semi-reviewing-hiatus">Mario the lone bookwolf (semi reviewing hiatus ) </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/78485297-mario-the-lone-bookwolf-semi-reviewing-hiatus?shelf=banks-m-iain">banks-m-iain</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2319567412" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15950424504267522423">That´s nothing more than one of the best allegories on human culture ever written, described from the point of view of an objective observer of a far higher developed civilization.<br/><br/>I had the luck to read this, probably his best novel, as one of the first out of Banks´ amazing universe and in contrast to the other, often very complex, eclectic and multi-plotted novels it stays focused on the main premise to show us how freaking average and dull we are. I guess Banks did it on purpose, as a stylist</span>
  <span id="freeText15950424504267522423" style="display:none">That´s nothing more than one of the best allegories on human culture ever written, described from the point of view of an objective observer of a far higher developed civilization.<br /><br />I had the luck to read this, probably his best novel, as one of the first out of Banks´ amazing universe and in contrast to the other, often very complex, eclectic and multi-plotted novels it stays focused on the main premise to show us how freaking average and dull we are. I guess Banks did it on purpose, as a stylistic element, to say much with less and because it might have seemed inappropriate and weird to mix present history with the lighter space opera elements and humor of his other novels.<br /><br />To integrate "The game" as an element of selection in an authoritarian government is a marvelous plot vehicle, looking at you, Hunger Games, Battle Royale, The Long Walk, etc., but mixing it with higher, superior entities that could wipe the floor with the dictators while optimizing quantum gravity time dilation multiverse theoretical physics stuff with the other, (and doing whatever with as many hands, tentacles (I know what some of you are thinking now, shame on you!),... as they wish to have and create gripping devices by telekinetic manifesting them with grey/green goo nanotech in nanoseconds. nano nano nano) makes it both entertaining and insightful.<br /><br />Although it might be unrealistic that any evil despots might take the risk of participating in unfaked, unmanipulated competitions instead of letting the suppressed population kill each other in epic battles to keep them calm Roman emperor style. Except the tech is so highly advanced and secure and the probability of black swans so unlikely that they come down from their throne from time to time to slay their own people directly and under frenetic applause instead of conventionally killing them with secret police and incompetent agriculture politics.<br /><br />Just this moment I am realizing for the first time that Banks could be compared to Stanislaw Lem, another author that dived so marvelous and smooth into the depts of human nature. Of course, Lems´ complexity is unreached and the space opera focus makes the comparison difficult in some regard, but the authors' main intentions seem similar to me.<br /><br />Tropes show how literature is conceptualized and created and which mixture of elements makes works and genres unique: <br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://tvtropes.org/pmwiki/pmwiki.php/Literature/ThePlayerOfGames">https://tvtropes.org/pmwiki/pmwiki.ph...</a><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="15950424504267522423" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1312401127" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16724321037872819203">A good book is entertaining, tells an interesting story, and occupies your mind while you’re reading it. A great book does those things, but also changes you, changes the way you think about things, changes the things you think about. When you finish it you’re not the same person you were at the start. The Player of Games had this kind of effect on me. This book is a Trojan horse.<br/><br/>When I’m heavily invested in a book, I tend to fit in a chapter everywhere I can, often alternating between the physi</span>
  <span id="freeText16724321037872819203" style="display:none">A good book is entertaining, tells an interesting story, and occupies your mind while you’re reading it. A great book does those things, but also changes you, changes the way you think about things, changes the things you think about. When you finish it you’re not the same person you were at the start. The Player of Games had this kind of effect on me. This book is a Trojan horse.<br /><br />When I’m heavily invested in a book, I tend to fit in a chapter everywhere I can, often alternating between the physical book and audiobook depending on the situation I find myself in. I remember reading The Player of Games for the first time amid a period of domestic responsibility, with not much uninterrupted time to sit down with a book. I particularly remember listening to the audiobook while walking rows of blueberries on a small farm in Tontitown Arkansas, hoping to pick a gallon’s worth of berries early in the morning before the glaring sun had a chance to bake my skin. <br /><br />I don’t remember how picked-over the rows were that year, or how the blueberries tasted that season. I was too enraptured with that angry, sneaky little drone; heavily intrigued by the ins and outs of life on this Culture orbital; trying to figure out who the narrator was, what game they were playing at, and with whom. <br /><br />If I had a gun to my head and were forced to pick a favorite novel, it would be this one. I adore The Player of Games and reread it every few years. Each time it feels ripe with new detail and interpretive possibility, but it’s also just a great story. <br /></span>

          </span>

<div id="review_107602292" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/107602292" />
    <a title="mark monday" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/131922-mark-monday"><img alt="mark monday" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1572672245p2/131922.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/107602292?book_show_action=true">Jun 17, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="mark monday" class="user" itemprop="url" name="mark monday" href="/user/show/131922-mark-monday">mark monday</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/131922-mark-monday?shelf=scifi-60s-70s-80s">scifi-60s-70s-80s</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/131922-mark-monday?shelf=z-iain-banks">z-iain-banks</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/131922-mark-monday?shelf=x-culture">x-culture</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer107602292" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6306109072991230298">UPDATED REVIEW, 2nd read in 2015:<br/><br/>even more ingenious the second time around. <br/><br/>The Player of Games is taken to the Empire of Azad to play the greatest of games. the game is Azad is the Empire of Azad is the U.S. and the U.K. and all such toxic empires. in a civilized culture, all empires must fall. the game is feints and surprises and moves within moves; the game is the past that must be broken on the wheel of the future. Banks brings all of his customary elegance, intelligence, humor, and angry f</span>
  <span id="freeText6306109072991230298" style="display:none">UPDATED REVIEW, 2nd read in 2015:<br /><br />even more ingenious the second time around. <br /><br />The Player of Games is taken to the Empire of Azad to play the greatest of games. the game is Azad is the Empire of Azad is the U.S. and the U.K. and all such toxic empires. in a civilized culture, all empires must fall. the game is feints and surprises and moves within moves; the game is the past that must be broken on the wheel of the future. Banks brings all of his customary elegance, intelligence, humor, and angry frustration at the stupidity and short-sightedness of humanity. he understands the allure but still seethes at the very thought of brutality, let alone brutality as an ingrained governmental program or system. or as a way of life, for any so-called human. much like Banks, I am on the side of the AIs.<br /><br /><br />UGLY OLD REVIEW, 1st read in 2010:<br /><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[<br />an often brilliant allegory. it is interesting to compare the rather spare quality of this novel with the more luxurious expansiveness of the rest of the Culture novels... almost as if it is Iain without-the-M Banks writing about the Culture this time. and the themes are very much in line with banks' non-science fiction suspense novels. banks' wit and imagination are still in play. as are the wonderful drones! well, one drone in particular.<br /><br />mea culpa: so i have been recommending that folks start the Culture series from the beginning. perhaps this is entirely due to reading Consider Phlebas more recently and seeing how much sense it makes as the first novel of an incredible series. well, Player of Games was actually my own first Culture novel, and it worked out fine for me in the long run. so, whatever. choose whichever Culture novel you want to start off with.<br /><br />the challenge that i had with Player was its feeling of sparseness, when compared to the often over-stuffed feeling i get with more traditional space operas...and that nearly too-rich feeling is exactly what i'm usually looking for. i want that swarming of detail and incident, i want to be plunged into some richly imagined world-building. Player did not have that for me. i recognized its brilliance, but that brilliance was in a more intellectual mode, not one that i responded to emotionally or viscerally or as a means of escape into a completely realized yet often rather standardized universe. this is far from a critique (how could a person ever promote the rote and predictable? never!)... but it also did not exactly inspire me to keep reading Culture novels. after Player, it took some time for me to get back into the series. perhaps the escapist in me longed for a less rigorously intellectual pastime. or perhaps something that was less about aliens written like humans and more about actual aliens.<br /><br />still... great book. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span></span>
  <a data-text-id="6306109072991230298" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_29821819" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

    <a title="Stephen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1413439-stephen"><img alt="Stephen" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1311616811p2/1413439.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer29821819" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15538033345360587868"><b>Tis Official</b>...Iain Banks can <b>write</b> his <b>flesh cushion</b> off. Okay, so for many of you that is not exactly <b>breaking-news</b> scrolling across the <b>ticker</b>, but I still thought it was worth repeating.<br/><a href='http://s153.photobucket.com/albums/s207/sullypython/Animals/?action=view&current=100_2743-1-1.jpg' target='_blank' rel='nofollow noopener'>
  <img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380315172i/689101.jpg' alt='Photobucket' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/>
</a><br/><br/>I had previously read and loved <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/567678.The_Wasp_Factory' title='The Wasp Factory by Iain M. Banks' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Wasp Factory</a>, Banks' classic first novel which was a fascinating glimpse into the psychology of a very disturbed young man in serious need of a hug. I also really enjoyed <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/8935689.Consider_Phlebas__Culture___1_' title='Consider Phlebas (Culture, #1) by Iain M. Banks' rel='nofollow noopener'>Consider Phlebas</a>, which is the first of the Culture novels. With Banks having two big wins under hi</span>
  <span id="freeText15538033345360587868" style="display:none"><b>Tis Official</b>...Iain Banks can <b>write</b> his <b>flesh cushion</b> off. Okay, so for many of you that is not exactly <b>breaking-news</b> scrolling across the <b>ticker</b>, but I still thought it was worth repeating.<br><a href="http://s153.photobucket.com/albums/s207/sullypython/Animals/?action=view&amp;current=100_2743-1-1.jpg" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380315172i/689101.jpg" alt="Photobucket" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><br>I had previously read and loved <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/567678.The_Wasp_Factory" title="The Wasp Factory by Iain M. Banks" rel="nofollow noopener">The Wasp Factory</a>, Banks' classic first novel which was a fascinating glimpse into the psychology of a very disturbed young man in serious need of a hug. I also really enjoyed <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/8935689.Consider_Phlebas__Culture___1_" title="Consider Phlebas (Culture, #1) by Iain M. Banks" rel="nofollow noopener">Consider Phlebas</a>, which is the first of the Culture novels. With Banks having two big wins under his belt, I went into this second installment of the Culture series with fairly high expectations and that always makes me nervous and twitchy. It seems that whenever I go into a book hoping for mega, I more often than not crawl away from it feeling like....like um....kinda like uh....<br><a href="http://s153.photobucket.com/albums/s207/sullypython/Goodreads/?action=view&amp;current=kick-in-the-nuts-1.jpg" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380315172i/689102.jpg" alt="Photobucket" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br>Yeah...just like <b>THAT</b>!!!! <br><br>Well I'm a pleased as punch happy camper to report that there was no nut-crushing disappointment encountered during this read and Iain came through in fine fashion in this sophomore Culture novel. <br><br><b><u>BACKGROUND</u>:</b><br><br>Briefly, the Culture is an extremely advanced, post-scarcity, inter-galactic, utopian civilization. It is a symbiotic union between humans and god-like AI machines, with the AIs performing the administrative and governing functions (i.e., basically ruling) while humans live a leisurely existence enjoying the benefits of UNLIMITED RESOURCES. There are no laws, little reason for internal conflict and force is rarely needed and used only when necessary to protect people from harm. It is basically a giant, all-expenses paid, never-ending vacation in the most amazing high-tech resort you can imagine where the citizens of the culture get to eat....drink....<br><a href="http://s153.photobucket.com/albums/s207/sullypython/Gifs/?action=view&amp;current=family_guy_animation.gif" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1380315172ra/689103.gif" alt="Photobucket" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br>sex it up....be pampered like royalty....and explore all manner of hedonistic entertainment. <br><a href="http://s153.photobucket.com/albums/s207/sullypython/Gifs/?action=view&amp;current=bromance.gif" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1380315172ra/689104.gif" alt="Photobucket" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><br>In fact, because of the utopian nature of the Culture, everyone is pretty “kumbaya” and there is little to zero tension within the Culture itself. I know, I know…<b>DUH!!</b> <br><a href="http://s153.photobucket.com/albums/s207/sullypython/Goodreads/?action=view&amp;current=captain_obvious-1.jpg" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380315172i/689105.jpg" alt="Photobucket" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br>Therefore, the Culture novels mainly deal with either individuals outside of the Culture or with the Culture's efforts to expand its influence over a non-Culture society. Despite the many positive qualities of the Culture, they will definitely cut “ethical corners” and take a very “ends justify the means” approach to bringing other societies civilizations under their benevolent rule. <br><a href="http://s153.photobucket.com/albums/s207/sullypython/Decorated%20images/?action=view&amp;current=spreadingdemocracy.jpg" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380315172i/689106.jpg" alt="Photobucket" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><br><b><u>PLOT SUMMARY</u>:</b><br><br>The Player of Games deals with just such a situation. The main character is Jernau Morat Gurgeh who is among the greatest “game players” in all of the Culture. Through his numerous bio-enhancements (another perk of the Culture), he has mastered 1000s and 1000s of games and can absorb and master new ones incredibly fast. Well, this is just the kind of skill that the Culture’s <b>“Special Circumstances”</b> needs at the moment. I would describe Special Circumstances (SC) as a cross between the CIA and the State Department because they both investigate and establish ties with other cultures in order to learn their customs so they can then determine how best to manipulate them into joining the Culture. <br><br>It is seriously sweet. <br><br>Well SC wants Gurgeh to employ his talents to learn a new game. There is a massive civilization called the Empire of Azad that derives its name from an incredibly complex game called…uh… Azad.<br><a href="http://s153.photobucket.com/albums/s207/sullypython/superheroes%20and%20villains/?action=view&amp;current=captainobvious-30190.jpg" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380315172i/689107.jpg" alt="Photobucket" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a> <br><br>This game is central to the entire structure of the Empire's society and is so incredibly complex and nuanced that it takes a lifetime to be able to play. However, SC hopes that Gurgeh’s special aptitude will allow him to learn the game in just over two years (the travel time to the Empire). <br><br>That should be enough background and I will stop there so that I don’t spoil any of the central plot for you. Banks’ writing is top-notch and his imagination is exceptional as he provides a ton of details about life in and out of the Culture without allowing the pacing to get bogged down in a whole lot of exposition. He controls his story very well and you can be confidant that you are in capable hands. <br><br>This is space opera done very well by someone who has the writing chops to actually convey the wonder of his imagination to those of us who can only envy his talents. <br><br>4.0 to 4.5 stars. HIGHLY RECOMMENDED!! <br></span>
  <a data-text-id="15538033345360587868" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer18077480323901508190">In 1938, Yasunari Kawabata, a future Nobel Prize winner, was assigned by the Mainichi newspaper to cover a Go match between Honinbo Shusai, the top player, and his challenger Kitani Minoru. Go has an importance in Japanese culture that is hard for a Westerner to understand, and was one of the four traditional arts that a Samurai had to excel in. The match was very even until Kitani played an unexpected move just before an adjournment; its only purpose was to force a response, giving him extra ti</span>
  <span id="freeText18077480323901508190" style="display:none">In 1938, Yasunari Kawabata, a future Nobel Prize winner, was assigned by the Mainichi newspaper to cover a Go match between Honinbo Shusai, the top player, and his challenger Kitani Minoru. Go has an importance in Japanese culture that is hard for a Westerner to understand, and was one of the four traditional arts that a Samurai had to excel in. The match was very even until Kitani played an unexpected move just before an adjournment; its only purpose was to force a response, giving him extra time to think about his next play. This is completely standard practice in chess, but, although permitted by the rules of Go, was contrary to the complicated etiquette of the game. <br /><br /><b>The rest of this review is available elsewhere (the location cannot be given for Goodreads policy reasons)</b> <br /><br /></span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_89363273" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/89363273" />
    <a title="unknown" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2863914-unknown"><img alt="unknown" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1579703867p2/2863914.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/89363273?book_show_action=true">Feb 12, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="unknown" class="user" itemprop="url" name="unknown" href="/user/show/2863914-unknown">unknown</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780316005401" href="/book/show/1260578.The_Player_of_Games">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2863914-unknown?shelf=2011">2011</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2863914-unknown?shelf=series">series</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2863914-unknown?shelf=culture">culture</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2863914-unknown?shelf=wssfbc">wssfbc</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2863914-unknown?shelf=book-club">book-club</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2863914-unknown?shelf=sci-fi-fantasy">sci-fi-fantasy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2863914-unknown?shelf=30-day-book-challenge">30-day-book-challenge</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2863914-unknown?shelf=vote-getters">vote-getters</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2863914-unknown?shelf=kindle">kindle</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer89363273" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4512093372712218695">This was my first book in Iain M. Banks sprawling Culture series. I have been reading a lot of sci-fi and fantasy lately, because for some reason that's all that sounds interesting to me, but I have to admit it is very annoying knowing that every book I pick up is the first in a _______. Usually that blank is "trilogy," except when it <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/series/45895-shadows-of-the-apt' rel='nofollow noopener'>isn't</a> (or it <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/series/40380-vorkosigan-saga' rel='nofollow noopener'><i>really</i> isn't</a>). And while there may be lots and lots of Culture books, they are all standalone stories with a beginning and an end. You can read one pu</span>
  <span id="freeText4512093372712218695" style="display:none">This was my first book in Iain M. Banks sprawling Culture series. I have been reading a lot of sci-fi and fantasy lately, because for some reason that's all that sounds interesting to me, but I have to admit it is very annoying knowing that every book I pick up is the first in a _______. Usually that blank is "trilogy," except when it <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/series/45895-shadows-of-the-apt" rel="nofollow noopener">isn't</a> (or it <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/series/40380-vorkosigan-saga" rel="nofollow noopener"><i>really</i> isn't</a>). And while there may be lots and lots of Culture books, they are all standalone stories with a beginning and an end. You can read one published in 1987 and one published in 2010 and it won't make a difference. This is very soothing to my nerves. <br><br>So anyway, the Culture. I wanted to read this series because of a Goodreads review I came across for <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/12013.Excession__Culture___5_" title="Excession (Culture, #5) by Iain M. Banks" rel="nofollow noopener">Excession</a> which noted that half the book is smartass back-and-forth between two sentient artificial intelligences. I love stories about wiseacre supercomputers; in my book, HAL 9000 is the hero of 2001: A Space Odyssey and all the humans just get in the way of the computer in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/16690.The_Moon_is_a_Harsh_Mistress" title="The Moon is a Harsh Mistress by Robert A. Heinlein" rel="nofollow noopener">The Moon Is a Harsh Mistress</a>. My favorite episode of Futurama is the one where the ship (voiced by Signouney Weaver, natch) falls for Fry (<i>"You're just jealous! Nobody loves you because you're tiny and made of meat!"</i>).<br><br>The Culture is a society ruled by these machines, which instead of going the violent Skynet route... <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380331951i/692772.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>has decided that hey, humans aren't so bad after all. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380331951i/692773.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>In the Culture, the machines take care of everything; no human goes hungry, disease and famine are a thing of the past. Sci-fi nerds call this a <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Post_scarcity" rel="nofollow noopener">post-scarcity</a> society, but basically it means that people don't have to actually <i>do</i> anything to survive. They don't even need to work, because no one needs money in a society with no wants. So basically because you are still going to need to do something with your existence, the human citizens of the Culture devote themselves to creative pursuits like art or repeatedly undergoing sex changes or, like Gurgeh, playing games.<br><br>Gurgeh is, in fact, the best Player of Games in the entire Culture. Board games, we're talking. Not sports. For this he is super-famous anyway, and frequently hosts parties, writes papers and speaks at symposiums. This would be like if the nerds who play Magic: The Gathering were as idolized as Magic: The Johnson. But Gurgeh is so good at all the existing games that he jumps at the chance to travel to a newly-discovered alien society known as The Empire (subtle!) and play the game known as Azad, which is so complex and revered that it has come to form the basis of the Empire's power structure. Meaning it would probably piss some people off if a foreigner came by and casually won, thus destroying the foundation of their entire society and such (symbolism that I totally missed is revealed in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/38295014" rel="nofollow noopener">Manny's review</a>).<br><br>That's a pretty good setup right there, I think. I like stories about games (the obvious parallel is, of course, Ender's Game), and this is a good one, even though Banks doesn't really explain Azad to us (this is just as well; it takes Gurgeh over a year of dedicated study to <i>begin</i> to understand the rules; reading them would be confusing/boring/underwhelming/all three). We don't have any idea what is going on, but the loosely sketched matches still make for exciting reading, as do the sometimes heavy-handed comparisons between the refined politeness of the Culture and the raw barbarism of the Empire, as well as the musings on the morality of state-building, i.e. intervening in a less advanced society because you know better, i.e. the Prime Directive Paradigm).<br><br>But what really made the book fun for me were the trappings of the Culture itself. The idea of a post-scarcity society is really interesting to me, and Banks has fashioned a good one, with a lot of fun examples of the ways humanity (so to speak) has dealt with its status as a largely extraneous life form in the grand scheme of galaxy-spanning sentient worldships. The AIs themselves are collectively my favorite characters, from the massive spaceships, so big they are controlled by robotic hive minds, to the small drones that follow humans around and make fun of them. And swear. I imagined them like this, but sassier:<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380331951i/692774.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>I always liked that movie. I bet if I watched it again I would discover it really isn't very good, Jessica Tandy aside (Tandy power!).<br><br>Despite my series-stress, I am definitely going to read more Culture novels.<br><br><i>Facebook 30 Day Book Challenge Day 5: Book you wish you could live in.</i></span>
  <a data-text-id="4512093372712218695" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_326781110" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/326781110" />
    <a title="Apatt" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2457095-apatt"><img alt="Apatt" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1349239948p2/2457095.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/326781110?book_show_action=true">May 09, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Apatt" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Apatt" href="/user/show/2457095-apatt">Apatt</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2457095-apatt?shelf=sf-top-20">sf-top-20</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2457095-apatt?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer326781110" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11671158574849003277">My third <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/series/49118-culture' rel='nofollow noopener'>Culture</a> book, a series of epic space opera about a post-scarcity human society in the far future. If you are not familiar with this series you may want to read this <a href='http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Culture' rel='nofollow noopener'>Wikipedia entry</a> first and come back (or not, as you prefer). I love <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/8935689.Consider_Phlebas__Culture___1_' title='Consider Phlebas (Culture, #1) by Iain M. Banks' rel='nofollow noopener'>Consider Phlebas</a> but I followed that up with fan favorite <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/12007.Use_of_Weapons__Culture___3_' title='Use of Weapons (Culture, #3) by Iain M. Banks' rel='nofollow noopener'>Use of Weapons</a> and it nearly put me off the entire series. I don't want to go into why I do not like that book, if you are curious you can always find my review. Still, I love Consider Phlebas so much</span>
  <span id="freeText11671158574849003277" style="display:none">My third <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/series/49118-culture" rel="nofollow noopener">Culture</a> book, a series of epic space opera about a post-scarcity human society in the far future. If you are not familiar with this series you may want to read this <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Culture" rel="nofollow noopener">Wikipedia entry</a> first and come back (or not, as you prefer). I love <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/8935689.Consider_Phlebas__Culture___1_" title="Consider Phlebas (Culture, #1) by Iain M. Banks" rel="nofollow noopener">Consider Phlebas</a> but I followed that up with fan favorite <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/12007.Use_of_Weapons__Culture___3_" title="Use of Weapons (Culture, #3) by Iain M. Banks" rel="nofollow noopener">Use of Weapons</a> and it nearly put me off the entire series. I don't want to go into why I do not like that book, if you are curious you can always find my review. Still, I love Consider Phlebas so much Use of Weapons could not completely eradicate the goodwill I still have for Mr. Banks and the Culture series. The Player of Games then is the book that will make or break the rest of series for me. <br /><br />Make it is.<br /><br />The Player of Games is complex, intelligent yet easy enough to follow, none of that mucking about with multiple timelines or switching to and fro between "the present" and flashbacks in some weird reverse order sequence. The story simply revolves around a single protagonist Jernau Gurgeh, possibly The Culture's greatest games players. That is saying something given how important games are to the indolent citizens of The Culture who are supplied with every material thing they can possibly want. Gurgeh is approached by the "Special Circumstances", the Culture's secret service / black ops type organisation to take part in an "Azad" game tournament at The Azad Empire, a rival civilization just a few light years away. This game is so important that it is the cornerstone of The Azad Empire. The winner is elevated to the Emperor status. As to why the Special Circumstances want Gurgeh to take part in this tournament you will have to find out for yourself by reading the book. You can thank me later.<br /><br />The most fascinating feature of this book for me is the Azad game, it seems like a hyper-chess game with various card games and philosophy thrown in. Its is so complex it makes Quidditch look like Snakes &amp; Ladders. Though the author does not describe the game in so much detail that it would be playable if you had the mega-board, the pieces, the cards and other things to hand, the description is done so well that you can imagine such a game existing. As with the other Culture books I have read Banks has populated the novel with quite a few well developed characters, though most of them tend to be AI or wee robots ("droids"). The central character Jernau Gurgeh is complex and interesting though not particularly likable, a typical trait of Banks' protagonists it seems. Still, at least he is not a tough-as-nails anti-hero, which is getting a bit old for me, his extreme focus and obsession makes him quite vivid. I also love the humorous moments interspersed throughout the book, these are mainly based around an indignant droid in a clunky disguise. The grand finale which takes place on a planet regularly burned by a perpetual wave of fire is wonderfully exciting though little plot twist at the end is not particularly surprising. Iain Banks' prose style is as literary as ever and is a pleasure to read.<br /><br />This book has made me re-commit myself to reading The Culture series, I look forward to reading many more volumes.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11671158574849003277" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1239174784" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer11892071267040150111">If you're into stuff like this, <a href='https://manuelaantao.blogspot.pt/2017/07/gaming-all-nighters-player-of-games-by.html' rel='nofollow noopener'>you can read the full review.</a><br/><br/><br/>Gaming All-Nighters: "The Player of Games" by Iain M. Banks <br/><br/><br/>"All reality is a game. Physics at its most fundamental, the very fabric of our universe, results directly from the interaction of certain fairly simple rules, and chance; the same description may be applied to the best, most elegant and both intellectually and aesthetically satisfying games. By being unknowable, by resulting from events which, at the sub-atomic level, cannot </span>
  <span id="freeText11892071267040150111" style="display:none">If you're into stuff like this, <a href="https://manuelaantao.blogspot.pt/2017/07/gaming-all-nighters-player-of-games-by.html" rel="nofollow noopener">you can read the full review.</a><br /><br /><br />Gaming All-Nighters: "The Player of Games" by Iain M. Banks <br /><br /><br />"All reality is a game. Physics at its most fundamental, the very fabric of our universe, results directly from the interaction of certain fairly simple rules, and chance; the same description may be applied to the best, most elegant and both intellectually and aesthetically satisfying games. By being unknowable, by resulting from events which, at the sub-atomic level, cannot be fully predicted, the future remains make-able, and retains the possibility of change, the hope of coming to prevail; victory, to use an unfashionable word. In this, the future is a game; time is one of the rules. Generally, all the best mechanistic games - those which can be played in any sense "perfectly", such as a grid, Prallian scope, 'nkraytle, chess, Farnic dimensions - can be traced to civilisations lacking a realistic view of the universe (let alone the reality). They are also, I might add, invariably pre-machine-sentience societies.”<br /><br /> <br /> <br />In “The Player of Games” by Iain M. Banks<br /> <br /> <br /> <br /><br />“I… exult when I win. It’s better than love, it’s better than sex or any glanding; it’s the only instant when I feel… real.”<br /><br /> <br /> <br />In “The Player of Games” by Iain M. Banks<br /> <br /> <br /> <br />Some of the imagery in Bank’s novel concerning gaming strategies closely remind me of my own: “In all the games he’d played, the fight had always come to Gurgeh, initially. He’d thought of the period before as preparing for battle, but now he saw that if he had been alone on the board he’d have done roughly the same, spreading slowly across the territories, consolidating gradually, calmly, economically … of course it had never happened; he always was attacked, and once the battle was joined he developed that conflict as assiduously and totally as before he’d tried to develop the patterns and potential of unthreatened pieces and undisputed territory.” This means you know you’ll get a biased sort of review. Just so you’re warned.<br /> <br /> <br />If you're into SF, read on.</span>

<div id="review_85909809" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/85909809" />
    <a title="Dirk Grobbelaar" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3102740-dirk-grobbelaar"><img alt="Dirk Grobbelaar" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1545117991p2/3102740.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/85909809?book_show_action=true">Jan 20, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dirk Grobbelaar" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dirk Grobbelaar" href="/user/show/3102740-dirk-grobbelaar">Dirk Grobbelaar</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780316005401" href="/book/show/1260578.The_Player_of_Games">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3102740-dirk-grobbelaar?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3102740-dirk-grobbelaar?shelf=books-i-own">books-i-own</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer85909809" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13317651176928750888">This was the second Culture novel I'd read, after <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/8935689.Consider_Phlebas' title='Consider Phlebas by Iain M. Banks' rel='nofollow noopener'>Consider Phlebas</a>. I’m trying to read them in order. Well, publication order in any case.<br/><br/>So I’ll come right out and say it: if you are a fan of Space Opera you should be reading the <i>Culture</i> novels. They vary a lot, stylistically and thematically, but they’re all pretty damn cool and very, very clever. Banks managed to juggle sense of wonder elements with intrigue almost effortlessly. Not to mention some gnarly political commentary.<br/><br/>I read somewhere</span>
  <span id="freeText13317651176928750888" style="display:none">This was the second Culture novel I'd read, after <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/8935689.Consider_Phlebas" title="Consider Phlebas by Iain M. Banks" rel="nofollow noopener">Consider Phlebas</a>. I’m trying to read them in order. Well, publication order in any case.<br /><br />So I’ll come right out and say it: if you are a fan of Space Opera you should be reading the <i>Culture</i> novels. They vary a lot, stylistically and thematically, but they’re all pretty damn cool and very, very clever. Banks managed to juggle sense of wonder elements with intrigue almost effortlessly. Not to mention some gnarly political commentary.<br /><br />I read somewhere, someplace, sometime, that people have likened <i>The Player Of Games</i> to <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/375802.Ender_s_Game__Ender_s_Saga___1_" title="Ender's Game (Ender's Saga, #1) by Orson Scott Card" rel="nofollow noopener">Ender's Game</a>, but I'm not sure I agree. There is a 'game' element in both books, obviously (even the titles suggest that), but that was where the similarities ended for me. I have also learned (possibly from this same vague, indeterminate source) that there are those who look down their noses at <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/8935689.Consider_Phlebas" title="Consider Phlebas by Iain M. Banks" rel="nofollow noopener">Consider Phlebas</a>, stating that <i>The Player Of Games</i> is <b>by far</b> the better of the two, and that you might as well start your Culture journey right here. <br />Who can tell? This kind of thing is too darn relative, but I will say that <i>Consider Phlebas</i> was (for me) more fun to read. And, in fact, I’ve rated it higher than <i>Player Of Games</i>. So there. I’ve said it. It can’t be unsaid. <br />They’re actually two very different beasts. I just warmed better to the protagonist in <i>Phlebas</i>, possibly because I <i>perceived</i> <b>The Game Player</b> to be somewhat aloof and detached. No doubt an important aspect of his identity, so it isn’t really a criticism. <br /><br /><b>Nitpicking aside</b> (how the hell did I get onto that tangent?), this is still a great book. Because less-than-optimal Banks is still better than most stuff out there, and this is Banks in fairly-close-to-optimal mode. <i>The Player Of Games</i> introduces us to one Jernau Morat Gurgeh. He plays games. He's very, very good at it too. We’re not talking about chess or checkers, or even your favourite RPG, MMORPG, FPS or LARP (or any other of a million other acronyms), but <b>complicated and lengthy</b> affairs from a variety of different (spacefaring) cultures. Fascinating, albeit complex, stuff all round. Gurgeh is therefore known as ‘The Game Player’.<br />All is well, until he is invited to a (very) distant empire to play the game of Azad. At first glance this doesn't seem like so big a deal, but it soon becomes apparent that Azad and Empire politics are intertwined and inseparable to a disturbing degree. Without giving the game away (so to speak), I will only say that Gurgeh at last seems to have bitten off more than he can chew. On second thought, contrary to my statement above, this is possibly where the Ender comparisons originated…. The whole “playing for keeps” thing. But Mum’s the word!<br /><br />That is more or less the gist of it. Don’t worry, it’s much more exciting than I’m making it sound. Being Banks, it’s a lot of fun. There is a lot of intrigue and maneuvering, and the game sequences are proficiently portrayed. Why not five stars? Well, it doesn’t have the same sprawling feel that <i>Phlebas</i> had (which is something I appreciate in my Sy-Fy). This one is more contained, more hush-hush, more conniving.<br /><br />At this stage it seems safe to say that the <i>Culture</i> books are stand-alone, so there is no pressing need to read <i>Consider Phlebas</i> before this. But you might as well, because this is the good stuff when it comes to big league Space Opera.<br /><br />Recommended.<br /><br /><u>update</u><br /><br />As of 2018 there appears to be a TV series in development, for <i>Consider Phlebas</i> specifically. One can hope they don’t muck it up, and if they manage to get it right, one can hope they expand it to include the other Culture novels (such as this one) as well. </span>
  <a data-text-id="13317651176928750888" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer105168495" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12872185303935556160">If I had to pick a favorite of Iain Banks...well, I haven't read them all yet, and anyway I couldn't pick, because each one I read becomes a favorite for a different reason. This one is a fascinating study of a complex character, set in an insanely well-drawn world. If you're a gamer you will definitely appreciate this book on another level, so pick it up!</span>

<div id="review_570505039" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/570505039" />
    <a title="Bradley" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4213258-bradley"><img alt="Bradley" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1508811508p2/4213258.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/570505039?book_show_action=true">Mar 25, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bradley" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bradley" href="/user/show/4213258-bradley">Bradley</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4213258-bradley?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4213258-bradley?shelf=space-opera">space-opera</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer570505039" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2012944928664173598">Starting my second read today, for a group read with a great group of people.... and I've finished my second read.<br/><br/>I'm much more impressed with the novel on the reread than I was the first time, so I've bumped my stars up from 4 to 5, and I don't think I'm being generous at all. It deserved it.<br/><br/>My main problem with either reading was that I just didn't quite care with the whole overt premise of a game player. I'm a game player, myself, but reading about games that are completely foreign and strang</span>
  <span id="freeText2012944928664173598" style="display:none">Starting my second read today, for a group read with a great group of people.... and I've finished my second read.<br /><br />I'm much more impressed with the novel on the reread than I was the first time, so I've bumped my stars up from 4 to 5, and I don't think I'm being generous at all. It deserved it.<br /><br />My main problem with either reading was that I just didn't quite care with the whole overt premise of a game player. I'm a game player, myself, but reading about games that are completely foreign and strange with rules only obliquely intersecting any that I've ever known strikes me as pointless and strange. It strikes as much interest in me as, say, reading a novel about Hockey or American Football. My boredom is so palpable that even my dog can smell it on me.<br /><br />And then, there's the other side of this book, the one that reads like a jousting tournament, full of heavily laden knights with shifting alliances and champions for opposing kingdoms. That part is quite exciting. It only gets better because it's set in the Culture, the ultimate let's-all-get-along mega-spanning galactic anti-empire filled with all types of aliens and machine minds living with (pretty much) no coercion, unless, of course, a bit of finesse is "Really" required.<br /><br />And that's where we come into the story, and we get to play and be a piece on the board at the same time, feeling all the ups and downs, the close-calls, the frustration, the elation and the triumph. Often all in a single night, oft repeated, but never dull, and this is true for me even though, as I said, the idea revolves around a freaking game with which I have no real stake.<br /><br />Well, that's true, I guess, until later, but by then the stakes take on a completely different flavor, and the fall of galactic civilizations are at stake. (Well, one is at stake, anyway. If you're reading this for the first time, I'll let you discover which one I'm talking about.)<br /><br />I paid closer attention to the descriptions of settings and people, this time, and was pleasantly surprised to see how they matched pace with the games this time, especially the one with the Big Guy on the Flaming Planet. And of course, no author can beat the wonderful names of the Culture Ships.<br /><br />I am glad I read this a second time. I actually forced myself to really try and imagine the game, or at least make up some heavy approximation of it, and in the end it became just another worldbuilding exercise. A lot of us readers like to fill in the blanks and use our imaginations to build a living and breathing world out of the hints and implications of authors, and I think I failed to do that last time. I focused on the world and enjoyed that plenty, but then I forgot to focus on the game. If you don't read this novel with the explicit intent to get into the game, itself, rather than just the interesting characters, then you're missing out on more than half the novel.<br /><br />That might turn some people off, just as it threatened to turn me off, but I feel better for sticking with it. The novel became really quite awesome by the end, and not just a clever plot.<br /><br /><br /><br />If you're really interested in what I wrote a few years ago about the novel then, here's what I threw together:<br /><br />"The novel is surprisingly deep for a character to start out so shallow. A very different novel from the first Culture novel and a much more direct plot-line with just as much of a great touch when it comes to the ebb and flow of the story. Very amusing satire that is only given a light touch, thank goodness, and used primarily to raise the tension. All in all, great writing, even if I won't put the novel among my top 100, but definitely a good read."</span>
  <a data-text-id="2012944928664173598" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer774852648643197143">Use of Weapons was far superior, in plot and characterization. Player of Games offered no surprises especially if you have read other Culture novels. The plot twist is reminiscent of Ender's Game, and is alluded to in the very first sentence. The central game is never described, and therefore too vague of a concept to care about. Any exposition about the human condition, racism, and sexism were poorly entwined into the book, and did not fit naturally into the plot.</span>

    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Milda by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/5315803-york">York</a>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">

<span id="freeTextContainer12489439006486638034">Sorry to say but it didn’t really work for me. My main issue being that storyline only became interesting in the last 30% of the book.<br/>I appreciate intelligent prose, the humour and interesting world (at least on Culture’s side). I also liked the ending, hence 2 stars.<br/>The list of the things I didn’t like is unfortunately longer:<br/>Two thirds of this book is really slow. Nothing really happens – no danger, no conflict, no intrigue or mystery, nothing to hook you in and keep turning pages.<br/>Descriptions</span>
  <span id="freeText12489439006486638034" style="display:none">Sorry to say but it didn’t really work for me. My main issue being that storyline only became interesting in the last 30% of the book.<br />I appreciate intelligent prose, the humour and interesting world (at least on Culture’s side). I also liked the ending, hence 2 stars.<br />The list of the things I didn’t like is unfortunately longer:<br />Two thirds of this book is really slow. Nothing really happens – no danger, no conflict, no intrigue or mystery, nothing to hook you in and keep turning pages.<br />Descriptions – there are a lot of (often lengthy) descriptions of landscape, sunset, interior, ship compartment etc. that are irrelevant to the story. On top of already slow plot…<br />Main character – is not exactly likeable. Arrogant, selfish, almost wilfully ignorant of what is happening around him, worried about winning his games more than about people. I couldn’t care less about his reputation or whether he wins or loses. Normally I’m all for the grey characters – but un-relatable character in combination with un-engaging plot – leaves no drive to the story.<br />Main subject – games - who would have thought it could be boring. The games in this book are not simulations. They are more complex version of chess and card games. There is no explanation of how they work but plenty of descriptions. Now playing a game yourself is exciting, but imagine watching a chess match or a snooker without even understanding the rules (ZZZzzzz… Snore… Huh. What?!).<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="12489439006486638034" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_871447431" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/871447431" />
    <a title="Penny" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10165256-penny"><img alt="Penny" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1340093730p2/10165256.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/871447431?book_show_action=true">Mar 04, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Penny" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Penny" href="/user/show/10165256-penny">Penny</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/11316767-the-player-of-games">review of another edition</a>


          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Penny by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/4026072-kim">Kim</a>
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10165256-penny?shelf=bookclub">bookclub</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10165256-penny?shelf=scifi-and-fantasy-club-bookshelf">scifi-and-fantasy-club-bookshelf</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10165256-penny?shelf=favourites">favourites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10165256-penny?shelf=highly-recommended">highly-recommended</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10165256-penny?shelf=game-theory">game-theory</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10165256-penny?shelf=dark">dark</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10165256-penny?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10165256-penny?shelf=language">language</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10165256-penny?shelf=scifi-fantasy-club-2014challenge">scifi-fantasy-club-2014challenge</a>,

          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer871447431" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11171320046003922324">Well played Mr Banks. Well played.<br/><br/>I'm struggling to find the words to express my awe in the wake of finishing this book. I feel much as I'd imagine a wizened game player would watching true masters dance across the board. Unable to do so myself, but completely transfixed by the beauty and depth of their movements. <br/><br/>I don't think I can recommend this highly enough. It isn't necessary to have read <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/8935689.Consider_Phlebas__Culture___1_' title='Consider Phlebas (Culture, #1) by Iain M. Banks' rel='nofollow noopener'>Consider Phlebas</a> which is the first book in the Culture series. I've read half of it and had to stop t</span>
  <span id="freeText11171320046003922324" style="display:none">Well played Mr Banks. Well played.<br /><br />I'm struggling to find the words to express my awe in the wake of finishing this book. I feel much as I'd imagine a wizened game player would watching true masters dance across the board. Unable to do so myself, but completely transfixed by the beauty and depth of their movements. <br /><br />I don't think I can recommend this highly enough. It isn't necessary to have read <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/8935689.Consider_Phlebas__Culture___1_" title="Consider Phlebas (Culture, #1) by Iain M. Banks" rel="nofollow noopener">Consider Phlebas</a> which is the first book in the Culture series. I've read half of it and had to stop to read a book club book and haven't pick it up since though I'm not sure why. Kim really enjoyed this one and suggested I add it to my <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/challenges/1957-read-all-the-books" rel="nofollow noopener">challenge</a>. I'm so so glad I did!<br /><br />It started out a bit slowly, but it wasn't in any way dull or boring. We learn a lot about the Culture and how those who are born within it live. It's a fascinating society. Highly technologically advanced, they live in a nearly utopian world where each citizen is free to do whatever they find most enjoyable. <br /><br />The Empire by contrast is not as advanced nor as accommodating. It's a brutal place where people have little in the way of rights and the Emperor rules supreme. Interestingly they choose their ruler by means of a highly complex and competitive game called Azad. <br /><br />I couldn't help but draw parallels between the planet Ea and Earth. Of course this was the worst possible parts of Earth and humanity, but it was in my head from near the beginning. You learn more about Ea as the book progresses that makes your blood run cold and I wished I hadn't made that connection in my mind early in the novel. I don't know if it was intentional on the part of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/5807106.Iain_M__Banks" title="Iain M. Banks" rel="nofollow noopener"> Iain M. Banks</a> but it resonated deeply in me.<br /><br />The game theory aspect was fascinating. It's always been a subject that I find interesting and it was put to such good use here. (This next bit is a spoiler since it only comes out near the end, but I don't think it ruins any part of the story at all. I'm marking it anyway for those who are completely spoiler averse.) <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[I also particularly liked the way language was demonstrated to impact the way you think and feel. Speaking only the language of the Empire for an extended period of time had a noticeable effect on the protagonist which was a subtle but genius touch I thought.  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />Besides being brilliant it's also just a really fun ride. <br /><br />I'm really looking forward to reading more in the Culture series. I'll be thinking about this one for months yet!</span>
  <a data-text-id="11171320046003922324" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_459981056" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/459981056" />
    <a title="Megan Baxter" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5742086-megan-baxter"><img alt="Megan Baxter" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1310153693p2/5742086.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/459981056?book_show_action=true">Nov 20, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Megan Baxter" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Megan Baxter" href="/user/show/5742086-megan-baxter">Megan Baxter</a>
      </span>

        rated it

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">

<div id="review_1103226468" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1103226468" />
    <a title="Scott" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/36819318-scott"><img alt="Scott" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1485843897p2/36819318.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1103226468?book_show_action=true">Nov 10, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Scott" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Scott" href="/user/show/36819318-scott">Scott</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36819318-scott?shelf=favourites">favourites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36819318-scott?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1103226468" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4029867645366687384">Sometimes an author writes a novel so great that while you're reading it you realise you're holding not only a kickass book, but the promise of many more amazing stories to come.<br/><br/><i>The Player of Games</i> is one of those novels - the sort of book that gives you that rare, sweet premonition of a future filled with tens of hours of pure reading pleasure.<br/><br/>This novel is the second book in Iain M. Banks' Culture series and while <i>Consider Phlebas</i> kicked off Banks' famous universe it is <i>The Player of Games</i> tha</span>
  <span id="freeText4029867645366687384" style="display:none">Sometimes an author writes a novel so great that while you're reading it you realise you're holding not only a kickass book, but the promise of many more amazing stories to come.<br /><br /><i>The Player of Games</i> is one of those novels - the sort of book that gives you that rare, sweet premonition of a future filled with tens of hours of pure reading pleasure.<br /><br />This novel is the second book in Iain M. Banks' Culture series and while <i>Consider Phlebas</i> kicked off Banks' famous universe it is <i>The Player of Games</i> that marks its entry into the illustrious ranks of the all-time greatest science fiction scenarios.<br /><br />This is a novel of riotous and fascinating imagination.<br /><br />Protagonist Jernau Gurgeh is a citizen of the post-scarcity, AI/human civilisation known as The Culture. Across a vast society of ringworlds, planets and moon-sized starships The Culture is a utopia whose people are free to pursue whatever interest or obsession takes their fancy. Sport, learning, sex, whatever - you can push the limits to your heart's (and other organs'!) content.<br /><br />Gurgeh has used this freedom to become an obsessive who spends his life playing and mastering all forms of games. He's known for it, and regarded highly for it. It's fair to say that playing games is central to who he is. This innocuous hobby has, however, drawn the eyes of some of The Culture's shadier citizens.<br /><br />For the culture, as friendly and utopian as it is, likes to meddle in the fates of more barbarous civilisations via its covert-ops division, Special Circumstances. SC has taken an interest in Gurgeh and by taking advantage of his obsession with winning they are able to blackmail him into agreeing to complete a job for them.<br /><br />Gurgeh is pressed into travelling to a faraway empire, a society somewhat less utopian than The Culture that uses a series of games - where the stakes can be life and death - to determine who will be their next leader. Gurgeh is to enter these games as a Culture observer, under the close protection of his Special Circumstances AI drone, but of course, his role may be a little bigger than he anticipates...<br /><br />I won't divulge any more as I would hate to spoil your reading fun, and what fun you'll have! Bank's wit, so rare in an SF writer and liable to make you laugh aloud, is evident here, along with his gift for pulse-racing action sequences, allied to an enviable skill at building completely plausible and immersive worlds. <br /><br />This is a fantastic novel that had me daydreaming about the Culture for weeks. Read it, and prepare to lock yourself in a room with the brilliant series of books that came after it. Seriously, if you love SF, and you haven't read <i>The Player of Games</i> stop what you're doing, ignore your friends and family and get thee to a bookstore.<br /><br />My only regret with having read the entire Culture series is that, like an idiot, I greedily gobbled them up too quickly, saving none to be savored later. <br /><br />With Banks' passing the Culture series is prematurely over. This is a terrible loss, but in my opinion, we're very, very lucky to have what he had time to write.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4029867645366687384" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
